009                                                   A BSTRACT
010
011             Outlier detection (OD) has a vast literature as it finds numerous applications in
012             environmental monitoring, security, manufacturing, and finance to name a few.
013             Being an inherently unsupervised task, model selection is a key bottleneck for OD
014             (both algorithm and hyperparameter selection) without label supervision. There is
015             a long list of techniques to choose from – both classical algorithms and deep neural
016             architectures – and while several studies report their hyperparameter sensitivity, the
017
                literature remains quite slim on unsupervised model selection—limiting the effec-
                tive use of OD in practice. In this paper we present FoMo-0D, for zero/0-shot OD
018
                exploring a transformative new direction that bypasses the hurdle of model selection
019
                altogether (!), thus breaking new ground. The fundamental idea behind FoMo-0D is
020             the Prior-data Fitted Networks, recently introduced by Müller et al. (2022), which
021             trains a Transformer model on a large body of synthetically generated data from a
022             prior data distribution. In essence, FoMo-0D is a pretrained Foundation Model
023             for zero/0-shot OD on tabular data, which can directly predict the (outlier/inlier)
024             label of any test data at inference time, by merely a single forward pass—making
025             obsolete the need for choosing an algorithm/architecture and tuning its associated
026             hyperparameters, besides requiring no training of model parameters when given a
027             new OD dataset. Extensive experiments on 57 public benchmark datasets against
028
                26 baseline methods show that FoMo-0D performs statistically no different from the
                2nd top baseline, while significantly outperforming the majority of the baselines,
029
                with an average inference time of 7.7 ms per test sample.
030
031
032
      1    I NTRODUCTION
033
034   Outlier detection (OD) finds applications in many domains such as security, environmental moni-
035   toring, finance, and so on. This popularity brings along a large literature that offers a plethora of
036   detection algorithms to choose from given a new OD task. These techniques, however, exhibit several
037   hyperparameters (HPs) that need careful tuning to which they are often quite sensitive (Ma et al.,
038   2023). What makes it notoriously difficult to achieve effective OD performance in practice is model
039   selection (both algorithm and HPs) in the absence of any labels, as most tasks are unsupervised.1
040   In fact, while deep learning and modern architectures have revolutionized many areas of machine
041   learning (ML), it has not quite been the case for OD—mainly because deep OD models (Pang et al.,
042   2021) exhibit many more HPs (for architecture, regularization, and optimization) that detection
043   performance is sensitive to (Ding et al., 2022), as compared to classical methods with only a few HPs.
044   Large foundation models have stirred up most recent advances in ML, which are (pre-)trained on
045   massive amounts of data. The most notable progress has been in natural languages and vision, thanks
046   to the admirable quantity and quality of public text and image datasets. In contrast, public (benchmark)
047   datasets for OD is minuscule in comparison (Han et al., 2022; Zhao et al., 2021; Steinbuss and Böhm,
048   2021). Another obstacle for foundation models for tabular OD has been the non-shared feature spaces
049   of different datasets, unlike the shared pixel or word spaces for images and text.
050   Recently, the introduction of Prior-data Fitted Networks (PFNs) has marked a milestone as a new
051   approach to ML on tabular data (Müller et al., 2022). PFNs are based on Bayesian non-parametrics
052
          1
053         While semi-/supervised settings of OD exist, unsupervised OD is preferable in most domains for the capacity
      to detect novel/emergent types of anomalies, beyond just the known types.

066
067
      and meta-learning on large quantities of synthetically simulated data from a data prior. The key idea
      is to compute a posterior predictive distribution (PPD) for a test point given the training data as input
068
      context. To approximate the PPD, a Transformer (Vaswani et al., 2017) is pre-trained to mimic the
069
      PPD via simulating numerous training datasets from a (general, complex) data prior. For inference,
070   the fresh training set along with the test samples are passed to the (frozen) pre-trained PFN, which
071   outputs the predictions in a single forward pass, requiring no model training or model selection.
072   Variants of PFN are shown to match the performance of tree-based models on small classification
073   datasets (Hollmann et al., 2023) and in time series forecasting with limited data (Dooley et al., 2023).
074
      In this paper, we capitalize on these ideas and introduce FoMo-0D; a prior-data fitted Foundation
075
      Model for zero- or 0-shot Outlier Detection (for the “Fear of Missing out”-liers). The implication
076   and “gift” of PFNs for unsupervised OD goes beyond those for supervised learning: it helps bypass
077   not only model (parameter) training, but most importantly, the notoriously-hard task of model
078   (hyperparameter) selection altogether. As such, FoMo-0D unlocks zero-shot OD on a new dataset
079   without the need for any algorithm or HP selection. During inference, data is used only as input
080   context to FoMo-0D, and not for parameter training or HP tuning. Arguably, this is a potential game
081   changer for unsupervised OD, especially for practitioners. Figure 1 illustrates the new FoMo-0D
082   paradigm versus the typical OD setting.
083   In designing FoMo-0D, we simply use Gaussian mixture models as a simple yet effective tabular data
084   prior, to capture general and diverse inlier data distributions, following current literature (Hollmann
085   et al., 2023; Zhao et al., 2021). We combine these with simulated outlier types common in the
086   real-world; namely local and global subspace outliers (Steinbuss and Böhm, 2021). While the data
087   prior can be extended to comprise more complex data distributions (e.g. through the use of Bayesian
088   Neural Networks (BNNs; (Neal, 2012)) and Structural Causal Models (SCMs; (Pearl, 2009)) as in
089   (Hollmann et al., 2023)), and additional outlier types can be included (e.g. dependency, contextual,
090
      etc. outliers), as we show in the experiments, even with the relatively straightforward prior that we
      employed, FoMo-0D achieves remarkable performance. As shown in Table 1, FoMo-0D pretrained
091
      on synthetic datasets with up to 100 dimensions performs statistically no different from all 26 state-
092
      of-the-art baselines (all p-values > 0.2) on 46 benchmark datasets with dimensionality d ≤ 500,
093   while significantly outperforming the majority of the baselines (with p > 0.95) (see Appendix Tables
094   12.1&12.2). Further, FoMo-0D takes a mere average of 7.7 ms to infer a test sample since a new
095   dataset requires a single forward pass for inference and no training overhead.
096
      Our contributions: We summarize the main contributions of our work as follows.
097
098    • A Foundation Model for Tabular OD: We present FoMo-0D, the first foundation model for
099      zero-shot OD on tabular datasets. FoMo-0D is a Prior-data Fitted Network (PFN) (Müller et al.,
100      2022) that is pretrained on many synthetically generated datasets drawn from a novel data prior
101      that we introduce to capture various inlier and outlier distributions. The pretrained FoMo-0D can
102      then directly compute the posterior predictive distribution (PPD) of test points in a new dataset.
103    • Unsupervised Outlier Model Selection Made Obsolete: The most outstanding property of
104      FoMo-0D is its zero-shot inference on a new dataset via a single forward pass, fully abolishing
105      the need not only for model training on a new dataset, but importantly also the notorious task of
106      algorithm selection and hyperparameter tuning in the absence of labeled data.
107    • Scalable Pre-training Design: To unlock the premise of large-scale pretraining on numerous
         large datasets, (1) we implement a new mechanism to speed up sample-to-sample attention from


                                                                    2
      Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


108
         quadratic to linear time complexity—enabling larger datasets; and (2) we scale up on-the-fly data
109      synthesis through data transformation—enabling more datasets in less time.
110
       • Fast Inference at Detection Time: Thanks to a pretrained prior-data fitted Transformer, FoMo-
111      0D bypasses both model (parameter) training and selection, both of which can be slow for modern
112      deep OD models with many hyper/parameters. Rather, it takes fraction of a second to label a
113      test point through a single forward pass that can be parallelized across test samples. Such speedy
114      inference also unlocks the potential for deploying FoMo-0D in real time on data streams.
115    • Effectiveness: We evaluate FoMo-0D on 57 public benchmark datasets (Han et al., 2022) from
116      diverse domains and compare against 26 baselines from classical to modern (Livernoche et al.,
117      2024), where FoMo-0D significantly outperforms the majority of the baselines while performing
118      statistically no different from the top 2nd baseline, at the fraction of the compute cost.
119
      As FoMo-0D proposes a paradigm shift for OD, abolishing model training and selection altogether,
120
      while delivering unreasonable effectiveness on benchmark datasets even with a basic data prior, we
121   expect FoMo-0D will trigger further work in both research and practice. To this end, we make all of
122   our codebase for synthetic data generation, model training, and our pretrained FoMo-0D checkpoints,
123   openly available at https://anonymous.4open.science/r/PFN40D.

      2     P ROBLEM AND P RELIMINARIES
127
128
      2.1   S EMI - SUPERVISED O UTLIER D ETECTION
129
      Outlier detection (OD) methods can be categorized based on the availability of labeled data. In
130   supervised OD, the task is similar to binary classification with imbalanced classes (as outliers typically
131   make up only a small portion of the overall data). The more difficult unsupervised setting assumes
132   the “contaminated” training data contains both inliers and outliers, but without any labels. A semi-
133   supervised or one-class classification approach lies between these two extremes, where only inlier
134   data is available for training, but unknown outliers may appear during inference. Semi-supervised
135   OD is used in practice where it is easy to gather inlier data, but learning from known, labeled outliers
136   is undesirable because outliers are hard to collect and/or new, unknown outlier types are likely to
137   arise in future test data that renders learning only from the known outliers suboptimal/risky.
138   Note that semi-supervised OD may be a misnomer from the supervised ML perspective, where
139   semi-supervised classification assumes the presence of some labeled instances from all classes in
140   the training data. As such, model selection continues to be as difficult for semi-supervised OD as
141   unsupervised OD, where no labeled outliers exist in the input/training data in both settings.
142   We focus on semi-supervised OD. Formally, let Din = {(x1 , y1 ) . . . , (xn , yn )} denote the input data
143   containing only inliers xi ∈ Rd , where yi = 0 ∀i ∈ [n], and Dtest depicts the test data comprising
144   both inliers and outliers. The task is to assign labels to xi ∈ Dtest given the inlier-only input Din .
145
146   2.2   BACKGROUND ON P RIOR - DATA F ITTED N ETWORKS
147
148   Posterior Predictive Distribution (PPD): In the Bayesian framework for supervised learning, the
149   prior defines a hypotheses space Φ which expresses our beliefs about the data distribution before
150   seeing any data. Each hypothesis ϕ ∈ Φ describes a mechanism by which the data is generated. The
151   posterior predictive distribution p(·|xtest , Dtrain ) provides a framework for making prediction on
152
      new, unseen test data xtest , conditioned on observed training data Dtrain = {(x1 , y1 ), . . . , (xn , yn )}.
      Based on Bayes’ Theorem, the PPD can be derived by the integration over the space of hypotheses Φ:
153
                                                    Z
154
                        p(ytest |xtest , Dtrain ) =   p(ytest |xtest , ϕ)p(Dtrain |ϕ)p(ϕ)dϕ,                    (1)
155
                                                     Φ
156
157   where p(ϕ) denotes the prior probability and p(D|ϕ) is the likelihood of the data D given ϕ.
158   PFNs and PPD Approximation: As obtaining the above PPD is generally intractable, Prior-data
159   Fitted Networks (PFNs) are proposed to approximate the PPD (Müller et al., 2022). Unlike traditional
160   machine learning models that are trained directly on observed datasets, PFNs are pre-trained offline
161   on simulated datasets that are generated according to a prior distribution. Specifically, it contains the
      pre-training and inference stages described as the following.

174
              Figure 1: (best in color) Comparison of typical OD vs. the FoMo-0D settings. Given a new un/semi-
175
              supervised OD dataset, FoMo-0D not only eliminates the need for model training, but most importantly,
176           also abolishes the onerous task of model selection (algorithm and hyperparameters) w/out labels.
177
178           Pre-training on synthetic data. At the beginning of the pre-training stage, massive synthetic training
179           datasets are generated, by first sampling a hypothesis (i.e., the generating mechanism) ϕ ∼ p(ϕ), and
180           then sampling a dataset D ∼ p(D|ϕ). For training purposes, each dataset D can be split as Dtest ⊂ D
181           and Dtrain = D \ Dtest . Thus the PFN with parameters θ can be optimized by making predictions on
182           data points in Dtest . For a test point (xtest , ytest ) ∈ Dtest , the training loss is formulated as
183
184
                                     L = E({(xtest ,ytest )}∪Dtrain )∼p(D) [− log qθ (ytest |xtest , Dtrain )] .                                                (2)
185           The above loss can also be interpreted as minimizing the expected KL divergence between p(·|x, D)
186           and qθ (·|x, D) (Müller et al., 2022). In practice, a PFN model qθ is typically implemented by a
187           Transformer-based architecture (Vaswani et al., 2017), which takes (xtest , Dtrain ) as input, where
188           xtest ∈ Dtest and Dtrain contains an arbitrary number of instances. The output is the conditional class
189           probabilities for xtest . As the whole training set Dtrain is passed as input/context to the Transformer,
190           it learns to predict class labels through sample-to-sample attention.
191           Inference on real-world data. In the inference stage, a fresh real-word dataset Dtrain and some test
192           instance xtest are fed into the (frozen) pre-trained model, which computes the PPD qθ (·|xtest , Dtrain )
193           in a single forward process. Importantly, PFNs do not require gradient-based parameter tuning on
194           data observed at inference time, where the training and prediction are delivered through a one-step
195           forward process in less than a second (Hollmann et al., 2023).
196
              In summary, PFNs are trained once offline, and can be used many times for zero-shot inference when
197           new datasets with different characteristics are input. The main benefit is that no training or tuning
198           is required at the inference stage. This type of learning ability is also termed as in-context learning
199           (ICL) (Xie et al., 2021), which was shown to be an effective paradigm for various tasks in NLP with
200           the stream of large language models (Brown et al., 2020). In fact, ICL with PFNs is recently shown
201           to be a promising paradigm for supervised classification on tabular datasets (Hollmann et al., 2023).
202
203
204
              3      FoMo-0D: A N EW PFN FOR 0- SHOT OD – M ODEL S ELECTION B YGONE !
205
206
              Inspired by the recent PFNs (Müller et al., 2022) and their successful applications in supervised
              classification (Hollmann et al., 2023) and time series forecasting (Dooley et al., 2023), we propose
207
              FoMo-0D, a prior-data fitted Foundation Model for 0-shot Outlier Detection. FoMo-0D is (pre)trained
208
              on a large body of synthetically generated OD datasets toward zero-shot inference on a new dataset.
209           Most notable of our zero-shot FoMo-0D is its elimination of the need not only for model training on a
210           new dataset, but especially also for model selection (both algorithm and HPs), which is notoriously-
211           hard without any labeled data. By breaking such new ground, and its effectiveness on many benchmark
212           datasets compared to classical and modern baselines, we expect FoMo-0D will become a milestone in
213           future research and practice of OD. The new FoMo-0D paradigm (right) versus the typical OD setting
214           (left) is illustrated in Figure 1.
215           In the following we describe our OD data prior, training of FoMo-0D on prior-simulated datasets,
              inference on new datasets, and our specific model architecture and improvements for scalable training.

                                                                                            4
      Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


216
      3.1   D ESIGNING A DATA P RIOR FOR O UTLIER D ETECTION
217
218   Arguably, what has triggered the recent breakthroughs in NLP and CV is the massive amounts of
219   datasets available for (pre)training, along with high-capacity model architectures. In comparison
220   to the natural language and image domains, the quantity (and quality) of publicly available tabular
221   OD datasets is minuscule. Even in the presence of large quantities of data, in training their Chronos
222   foundation models for time series forecasting, Ansari et al. (2024) show that using synthetic data in
223   combination with real-world data improves the overall zero-shot performance. For these reasons, we
224
      design a new data prior from which we simulate numerous OD datasets for pretraining FoMo-0D.
225   Ideally the data prior should reflect distributions as general and diverse as seen in real-world datasets,
226   however, “finding a prior supporting a large enough subset of possible [data generating] functions
227   isn’t trivial” (Nagler, 2023). Surprisingly, in contrast, our initial attempt has been sufficient to achieve
228   remarkable performance even with a relatively straightforward and simple-to-implement data prior,
229   which we describe next.
230   Inlier synthesis: We simulate inliers by simply drawing from a Gaussian Mixture Model (GMM)
231   with m-clusters in d-dimensions, with centers µjk ∈ [−5, 5], j ∈ [m], k ∈ [d] and diagonal2 Σj with
232   entries in [−5, 5]. In each step of every epoch during pretraining, we create batch size B different
233   GMMs with varying m ≤ M and d ≤ D chosen uniformly at random from [M ] and [D], respectively.
234   From each GMM, we draw a set of S inliers, defined as instances within the 90%-ile of the GMM.
235   Outlier synthesis: Following the previous literature on outlier synthesis (Han et al., 2022), we
236   generate subspace outliers by first drawing a subset of dimensions K at random, where |K| ≤ d, and
237   then generate S points from the corresponding “inflated” GMMs, which share the same centers µj ’s
238   with the original GMM but with the inflated (diagonal) covariances 5 × Σj,kk ’s for k ∈ K. Outliers
239   are defined as points outside the 90%-ile of the original GMM. We label each sample based on its
240   Mahalanobis distance computed analytically (see Property B.2 in the Appendix).
241   Specifically, we simulate datasets containing 2S = 10, 000 samples (half inlier, half outlier) from
242   the two corresponding GMMs (original and inflated) with up to M = 5 clusters and up to D = 100
243   dimensions. Example 2-d synthetic datasets are illustrated in Appendix A.
244
      Remarks: We emphasize once again that our model is not trained on any real-world data and rather,
245
      on purely synthetic data (although future work can combine existing benchmark OD datasets with
246   synthesized data, as was done for Chronos (Ansari et al., 2024)). Notably, our GMM-based data
247   prior can be seen as extremely basic. While it has been our intent to extend our preliminary attempt
248   toward designing a sophisticated data prior for OD, we found to our surprise that even with such an
249   elementary prior, FoMo-0D performs remarkably well against numerous SOTA baselines. Therefore,
250   we present FoMo-0D using this effortless approach for its simplicity to showcase the prowess of PFNs
251   for OD. Future work can employ BNNs and SCMs (Hollmann et al., 2023), and other outlier types
252   (contextual, dependency, etc. (Steinbuss and Böhm, 2021)) toward a more comprehensive data prior.
253
254   3.2   (P RE )T RAINING AND I NFERENCE
255
256   Model (Pre)Training (Once, Offline): FoMo-0D is a Prior-data Fitted Network (PFN, see Section
257   2.2) based on the Transformer architecture. In the synthetic prior-data fitting phase, it is trained
258   on datasets drawn from our OD data prior for tabular data that we introduced in Section 3.1. Each
259
      dataset is simulated from a different GMM configuration based on randomly drawn parameters, and
      consists of varying number of training samples and dimensions to capture the diversity in real-world
260
      tabular datasets. Detailed steps are outlined in Algo. 1 in Appendix C.2, and described as follows.
261
262   Each time, we first draw a hypothesis (i.e. GMM configuration) uniformly at random, that is
263   ϕ = {d ∈ [D], m ∈ [M ], {µj }m                  d       m                       d
                                       j=1 ∈ [−5, 5] , {Σj }j=1 ; diag(Σj ) ∈ [−5, 5] }, and then generate
264   a dataset D = {Din , Dout } containing synthetic inlier and outlier samples from the drawn hypothesis
265   and its variance-inflated variant, respectively.
266                                                                              in     out
      We optimize FoMo-0D’s parameters θ to make predictions on Dtest = {Dtest       , Dtest }, conditioned
267   on the inlier-only training data Dtrain ⊂ Din based on the cross-entropy loss (see Eq. (2)). During
268
         2
269        In our early experiments, we found no difference in terms of test performance on synthetic datasets between
      using diagonal and non-diagonal Σ, however, it is easier to compute the inverse of diagonal Σ for generation.


                                                             5
      Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


270                                                                                       in
      training, Dtest contains a balanced number of inlier and outlier samples, where Dtest   = Din \Dtrain ,
271          out
      and Dtest  ⊂ Dout contains an equal number of samples as Dtestin
                                                                        . To vary the training data size, we
272   subsample Dtrain of randomly drawn size n ∈ [nL , nU ], where nL and nU denote the lower and
273   upper bounds. In our current implementation, we set nL = 500, and nU = 5, 000.
274
      FoMo-0D is trained on 200, 000 batches (200 epochs × 1, 000 steps/epoch) of B = 8 generated
275
      datasets in each batch. While this pretraining phase can be expensive, it is done only once, offline.
276
      Moreover, we introduce several scalability improvements to speed up pretraining, as discussed later
277   in Section 3.3. Full details on the training and implementation of FoMo-0D are given in Appendix C.
278
279   Zero-shot Inference (on Unseen Dataset): During the inference phase, our pretrained-in-advance
280
      FoMo-0D can be employed on any unseen real-world dataset. In fact, we apply the same single
      pretrained network on all benchmark datasets in our experiments in this paper.
281
282   Specifically, for a new semi-supervised OD task with inlier-only training data Dtrain and mixed
283   test data Dtest , feeding ⟨Dtrain , xtest ⟩ as input to FoMo-0D (for each xtest ∈ Dtest separately) yields
284   the PPD qθ (y|xtest , Dtrain ) in a single forward pass. As such, FoMo-0D performs model “training”
285   and prediction simultaneously at test time. In fact, as the entire training data is passed as context,
286
      FoMo-0D leverages in-context learning (ICL) (Xie et al., 2021; Garg et al., 2022) for inference.
      The key contribution of FoMo-0D goes beyond eliminating gradient-based model training for a new
287
      dataset: because no model training is required, one thus neither needs to choose any specific OD
288
      model to train, nor grapple with tuning any hyperparameters of the said model—rendering model
289   selection an obsolete concern for the future of OD. Additionally, the speedy, easily parallelizable
290   inference (for less-than-a-second per test sample) is then the “icing on the cake”.
291
292
      For a visual summary, Figure 1 (right) illustrates (top) pretrain & (bottom) test phases of FoMo-0D.
293
294   3.3   A RCHITECTURE AND S CALABILITY
295
296   Architecture and sample-to-sample attention: Like existing PFNs in the literature, FoMo-0D is
297   based on the Transformer architecture (Vaswani et al., 2017), encoding each sample’s feature vector as
298
      a token, and allowing token representations to attend to each other, hence enabling sample-to-sample
      attention. We also adopt the three adaptations of TabPFN (Hollmann et al., 2023), which (1) computes
299
      self-attention among all the training samples but only cross-attention from test samples to the training
300
      samples, (2) enables variable feature dimensionality by zero-padding, and (3) randomly rotates input
301   samples while omitting positional encodings to achieve model invariance to sample permutations in
302   the dataset. We defer the architecture details to the original papers.
303
304
      Given Dtrain = {x1 , . . . , xn }, each self-attention layer outputs n embeddings {zi }ni=1 ; where the
      i-th token is mapped via linear transformations to a key ki , query qi and value vi based on which the
305
      i-th output is computed by weighing all vj ’s by the normalized dot product between qi and all the
306
      kj ’s (i.e. sample-to-sample dot product similarity) as
307                                       n
                                         X
308                                zi =     softmax( {⟨qi , kj′ ⟩}nj′=1 )j · vj .                          (3)
309                                    j=1
310
      The sample-to-sample attention is intriguing from the perspective of OD: Many classical OD algo-
311
      rithms (Aggarwal, 2013) are based on nonparametrics; in particular, they make use of the distances
312   to the k nearest neighbors (kNNs) of a point to compute its outlierness (where k is a critical hy-
313   perparameter (HP)). One can think of FoMo-0D as mimicking non-parametric models but by using
314   parametric attention mechanisms. Interestingly, PFNs are much more robust and flexible than kNN
315   based OD approaches, for (1) sample-to-sample relations are not pre-specified but rather learned
316   through attention weights, and thus (2) they are not limited to just the nearest neighbors but rather can
317   learn which training points are worth attending to, and last but not least (3) as attention is dataset-wide
318   across all points, there is no need for specifying a cut-off HP value like k, to which most kNN based
319   OD techniques are sensitive to (Aggarwal and Sathe, 2015; Campos et al., 2016; Goldstein and
320
      Uchida, 2016; Ding et al., 2022)—to reiterate, algorithm & HP selection is bygone with FoMo-0D.
321   While intuitively beneficial for OD, “vanilla” attention among the training samples incurs quadratic
322   complexity. To be able to seize the benefits with scale, we incorporate a scalable architecture to our
323   design, as we describe next. The scale up also unlocks a larger context (i.e. dataset) size for FoMo-0D,
      enabling its pretraining on larger datasets for potentially better generalization.


                                                          6
                   Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


         324
                   Scaling up attention with “routers”: The O(n2 ) quadratic sample complexity at pretraining
         325       presents an obstacle for achieving high performance at inference. From dataset size perspective,
         326       it limits pretraining to relatively small training datasets. From context size perspective, it limits
         327       in-context learning that typically benefits from longer context lengths (Xie et al., 2021).
         328
improve  329
                   Toward a high-performance pretrained model, we scale up FoMo-0D’s attention via the “router
                   mechanism” of Zhang and Yan (2023). As shown in Figure 2, the main idea is to learn a small number
         330
                   (R ≪ n) of “routers” or representatives, which gather information from all n samples and then
         331       distribute the information back to the n output embeddings, creating what-looks-like a “bottleneck”
        332        attention mechanism—reducing complexity from O(n2 ) to O(2Rn) = O(n). This design allows
        333        FoMo-0D training to scale linearly with respect to both dimensionality d and also dataset size n.
        334

                    Concretely, the representatives             Dtrainaggre-
                                                                       )                                             q ( |x test , train )

                              Ẑ   =  MSA   2 (Z,  M,   M)      .        (5) mechanism” for scalable attention.
         349       Finally, we obtain Z̄ = LayerNorm(Ẑ + Z) after layer normalization. Note that the test samples
         350       only attend to the training samples’ embeddings, computed in the described manner across layers,
         351       which finally feed into the prediction head for estimating each test sample’s PPD at the output layer.
         352       Scaling up (pre)training data synthesis with linear transforms: Besides the scalability challenge
         353       associated with architecture/attention, another computational challenge in pretraining FoMo-0D arises
         354       from drawing samples from the data prior. That is, generating samples from a pre-specified data                                     f
         355       distribution requires considerable time, especially in high dimensions3 , provided the large number of
         356       datasets we sample (concretely, a batch size of 8 datasets over 1,000 steps each for 200 epochs).
         357
                   To give an idea, sampling a dataset with n = 10, 000 points in d = 100 dimensions using 10 CPUs
         358
         359
                   in parallel takes ≈0.4 seconds (see Appendix Figure 7). Across 200 training epochs with 1,000 steps
                   each, it adds up to more than 177 hours just to generate 1,6 million datasets on-the-fly. Of course, one                   f(i )
         360       can trade storage with compute-time by generating all these datasets apriori via massive parallelism.
         361       Nevertheless, synthetic data generation demands considerable time (and/or storage).
         362
                   To scale up data synthesis, FoMo-0D employs two distinct strategies. First, we propose reuse at
         363
                   epoch level: that is, one can reuse the same 8K unique datasets at every epoch, or in general, the same
         364       8K×P datasets periodically at every P epochs. A larger P would lead to more diversity in terms of
         365       the overall pretraining data used.
         366
         367
                   Second, and more innovatively, we propose reuse at dataset level via transformation: that is, having
                   generated one unique dataset X ∈ Rn×d from a GMM, we propose a linear transform T (x) of the
         368
                   form Wx + b for randomly drawn parameters W ∈ Rd×d and b ∈ Rd (see Appendix B.1).4 This
         369
                   simple yet efficient transformation creates a new dataset, akin to one being drawn from another GMM
         370       with centers T (µj ) = Wµj + b and covariance T (Σj ) = WΣj WT , ∀j ∈ [m]. Note that we do
         371       not actually materialize these parameters but only transform the dataset. As we show in the following,
         372       such transformations preserve the Mahalanobis distances as well as the percentile thresholds for
         373       labeling points as inlier/outlier. Details and proofs are given in Appendix B.
         374           3
                         This is because the inverse of the (d × d) covariance matrix plays a crucial role in the process of generating
         375       samples from a GMM, which has O(d3 ) time complexity. (It is also partly the reason why we use diagonal Σj ’s
         376       in our data prior.) In addition, Mahalanobis distance for labeling inliers/outliers also requires the inverse.
                       4
         377             In practice, we apply the linear transform on the subspace of inflated features only, wherein inliers and
                   outliers are defined, which remains to be a multi-variate GMM.


                                                                              7
      Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025



                                                       d
378   Lemma 1 Linear transform T with invertible W on Gm preserves Mahalanobis distances.
379                                                    d
      Lemma 2 Linear transform T with invertible W on Gm preserves the percentiles of the GMM.
380
381   The implication of these lemmas is that a linear transformation of a dataset from a GMM retains the
382   identity of the inliers and outliers, i.e. no relabeling is required. Moreover, notice that as a byproduct
383   we obtain a transformed dataset as though it is drawn from a GMM with a non-diagonal covariance
384
      matrix which, besides the time savings, offers a slightly more complex data prior.
385   To reach 8K unique datasets for each epoch, we first generate 500 datasets from different GMMs (with
386   varying configurations), and then employ 15 different linear transformations to each unique dataset
387   by varying W and b. Drawing each (W, b) takes ≈0.02 seconds, while the matrix-matrix product
388   of X (n × d) and W (d × d) takes negligible time (for d ≤ 100). Thus, obtaining a transformed
389   dataset offers 20× speed-up compared to generating one (0.02 vs. 0.4 seconds).
390
391
      4     E XPERIMENTS
392
393   4.1   S ETUP
394
395
      We present the experiment setup briefly, including important notes on data synthesis, real-world
      datasets, baselines, metrics and HPs. For additional details, we refer to Appendix D.
396
397   Pre-training Dataset Synthesis: During pretraining, we generate unique GMM datasets by first
398   drawing a configuration, including dimensionality d ∈ [D], number of components m ∈ [M ], centers
399   {µj }m                        d                         m                     d
           j=1 (each µj ∈ [−5, 5] ) and covariances {Σj }j=1 (diag(Σj ) ∈ [−5, 5] ). We set M = 5
400   and vary D ∈ {20, 100} to study pretraining with relatively small and high dimensional datasets,
401   respectively. We synthesize inliers and outliers as described in Section 3.1.
402   Real-world Benchmark Datasets: While pretraining is purely on synthetic datasets, we evaluate
403   FoMo-0D on 57 real-world datasets from the ADBench benchmark (Han et al., 2022) (see Table 15).
404
      We use 5 train/test splits of each dataset via different seeds and report mean performance and standard
405
      deviation. Note that the baselines require model re-training and inference for each Dtrain /Dtest split,
406
      while FoMo-0D uses the splits only for inference as Dtrain is merely passed as context.
407
408   Baselines: We compare FoMo-0D against 26 baselines, from classical/shallow methods to mod-
409   ern/deep models. The baselines are imported from one of the latest papers that proposed the SOTA
410
      diffusion-based model DTE (Livernoche et al., 2024), and its three variants; DTE-C, DTE-IG, and
      DTE-NP. We defer to the original paper for additional details.
411
412   Model Implementation: We trained our final model for 200,000 steps with a batch size of 8 datasets.
413   That is, our FoMo-0D is trained on 1,600,000 synthetically generated datasets. This training takes
414   about 25 hours on 1 GPU (Nvidia RTX A6000). Each dataset had a fixed size of 10,000 samples,
415   with |Dtrain | ∈ [nL = 500, nU = 5000], and the rest used as Dtest with balanced number of inliers
416
      and outliers. Other implementation details of FoMo-0D, including the training algorithm, model
      architecture, data synthesis and reuse, and hardware are provided in Appendix C.
417
418   Metrics and Hypothesis Testing: Detection performance is w.r.t. 3 widely-used metrics for OD:
419   AUROC; area under ROC curve, AUPR; area under Precision-Recall curve, and F1 score; using
420   threshold at the true number of outliers in the test data (varies by dataset).
421   To compare methods, we compute their rank on each dataset (lower is better), and present average
422   rank across datasets. This is an alternative to the average metric (e.g. AUROC), which is not
423   meaningful when task difficulties and hence metric values vary widely. In addition, we perform
424   significance tests to compare two methods statistically, using the one-sided paired Wilcoxon signed
425   rank test (Demšar, 2006) between FoMo-0D and a baseline based on the performances across all
426   datasets and report the p-values. We consider results to be significant at 0.05 following convention.
427   Hyperparameters (HPs): Importantly, Livernoche et al. (2024) picked for each baseline the best-
428   performing set of HPs as recommended by the authors in their original paper. As for their own DTE,
429   which behaves similar to kNN, they use k = 5 and set the same k for the kNN baseline (Ramaswamy
430   et al., 2000) to be consistent. However, it is well known that kNN is sensitive to the value of k
431   (Aggarwal and Sathe, 2015), and so are many other OD models to their respective HPs (Campos
      et al., 2016; Goldstein and Uchida, 2016; Zhao et al., 2021; Ding et al., 2022).


                                                          8
      Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


432
      Therefore, we compare to the performance results of these baselines as imported from DTE’s Tables
433   13, 14 and 15, respectively for AUROC, F1, and AUPR (Livernoche et al., 2024). In addition, we also
434   compare to the top-45 best performing baselines (in order: DTE-NP, kNN, ICL, and DTE-C) on their
435   average performance across a list of different HP settings (which reflects their expected performance
436   under HP values selected at random, in the absence of any other prior knowledge), which is the
437   recommended approach by Goldstein and Uchida (2016) “to get a fair evaluation when comparing
438   [OD] algorithms”. We annotate the method name with avg for the version with performance averaged
439   over varying HPs. The detailed list of HP values for each top baseline is given in Appendix D.4.
440   Overall, we compare FoMo-0D to 30 baselines; 26 from Livernoche et al. (2024) and avg of the top-4.
441
442   4.2   R ESULTS
443
444   Detection performance: Table 1 presented the comparison of FoMo-0D w/ D = 100 to all
445   baselines w.r.t. average rank across datasets as well as pairwise Wilcoxon signed rank tests based
446   on AUROC (for full results on all datasets and all metrics, see Appendix G). We find that among 30
447   baselines and 2 variants of FoMo-0D (w/ D = 100 and D = 20), FoMo-0D w/ D = 100 performs as
448
      well as the 2nd best model (kNN with default HP; k = 5) on all datasets. While DTE-NP outperforms
      FoMo-0D with author-recommended k = 5, we find that DTE-NPavg is on par with FoMo-0D.
449
450   Against all other baselines, we obtain notably large p-values. Typically, p > 0.05 implies no statistical
451   difference between two methods. On the other hand, the large p-values we obtain that are often larger
452   than 0.50 suggest that the odds are tilted towards FoMo-0D to outperform.
453   FoMo-0D w/ D = 100 performs statistically no different from all baselines on datasets with d ≤ 100
454   (i.e., “at its own game” when pretraining data dimensions align with real-world datasets), while it
455   outperforms the majority of baselines (p > 0.95). These results also hold on datasets with d ≤ 500.
456
      Table 2 shows similar results for FoMo-0D w/ D = 20, which is pretrained on datasets with
457
      considerably fewer dimensions. Even in this limited setting, its performance is remarkable: against
458   30 baselines, it performs on par with the 3rd best baseline (ICL, with default HP). The p-value is
459   even larger (0.437) when compared to ICLavg . Moreover, on datasets with d ≤ 20 which align with
460   its pretraining data, all p-values are larger than 0.5, where it outperforms the top 5th baseline and the
461   majority of others. These are outstanding results for a model pretrained purely on synthetic datasets
462   from a simple data prior in small dimensions, showcasing the prowess of PFNs for OD.
463

      over 57 datasets is presented for FoMo-0D (with D = 20), top-10 baselines with default HPs, and
465
      top-45 baselines with performance avg.’ed over varying HPs (denoted w/ avg ); followed by p-values

      of the pairwise Wilcoxon signed rank test, comparing FoMo-0D to each baseline (from top to bottom)
467   over All (57) datasets, those (24) w/ d ≤ 20 and (38) datasets w/ d ≤ 50 dimensions, respectively.

469   the top 3rd baseline (ICL, w/ p = 0.089) across All datasets, while it outperforms the top 5th (LOF)
470   and onward baselines over datasets w/ d ≤ 20 (aligned w/ pretraining where D = 20) and d ≤ 50

520   4.3   A BLATION A NALYSES
521
522   Due to space limits, we present the detailed ablation analyses in Appendix E. We discuss the effect of
523
      D in E.1, the cost and performance of varying R in E.2 and E.3, the context size in E.4, the reuse
      periodicity P in E.5, the effect of data transformation T on performance and speed up in E.6 and E.7,
524
      data diversity and prolonged training in E.8, and quantile transformation on ADBench in E.9.

526
527   5     R ELATED W ORK
528
      Due to space limits, we present the detailed related work in Appendix J.
529
530
531   6     C ONCLUSION
532
      We introduced FoMo-0D, the first foundation model for outlier detection (OD) on tabular data. It
533
      capitalizes on the in-context learning ability of a Transformer model pretrained on a large number of
534   synthetic datasets that can then perform zero-shot inference on a new dataset by directly passing it as
535   input context. FoMo-0D breaks new ground by fully abolishing notoriously-hard model selection.
536   Further, FoMo-0D offers extremely fast inference thanks to a mere single forward pass. Against 26
537   baselines on 57 public datasets from diverse domains, FoMo-0D performs on par with the 2nd best
538   baseline, while significantly outperforming the majority of baselines. We leave improving the data
539   prior and extending beyond tabular OD, among others, as future directions. For a detailed discussion
      on limitations and future directions, we refer to Appendix K.

       B           L INEAR T RANSFORM FOR S CALABLE GMM DATA S YNTHESIS

       B.1         D EFINITIONS
781
782   Definition 1 (Gaussian Mixture Model) We denote an m-cluster d-dimension Gaussian Mixture
                 d
783   Model as Gm   = {(wj , µj , Σj )}m
                                       j=1 , which is the weighted sum of m Gaussian distributions:
784                                                                           m
785
                                                                              X
                                                                 p(x) =             wi · g(x|µj , Σj ) ,                                     (6)
786
                                                                              j=1
787                                                                         Pm
788   where wj ∈ R+ is the weight for the j-th Gaussian N (µj , Σj ) with j=1 wj = 1, and g(·|µj , Σj )
789   is the density of the j-th component/cluster, with mean/center µj ∈ Rd and covariance Σj ∈ Rd×d
790   being positive semi-definite, such that xT Σi x ≥ 0, for all x ∈ Rd .
791
792   Definition 2 (Linear Transform) We denote a linear transformation T in Rd as:
793
                                                                          T (x) = Wx + b ,                                                   (7)
794
795   where x ∈ Rd , and W ∈ Rd×d , b ∈ Rd are the parameters of T .
796
797   Definition 3 (Mahalanobis Distance) The Mahalanobis distance distM between a point x ∈ Rd
798   and a Gaussian distribution N (µ, Σ) is defined as:
799                                             q
800                                distM (x) = (x − µ)T Σ−1 (x − µ) .                        (8)
801
802   Definition 4 (χ2d -distribution) The Chi-squared distribution χ2d with d degrees of freedom is the
803   distribution of the sum of squares of d independent standard Normal random variables.
804

808   X1 , ..., Xd are independent and Xi ∼ χ21 , then i=1 Xi ∼ χ2d .
809
      Property B.2 The squared Mahalanobis distance dist2M (x) ∼ χ2d , with x ∼ N (µ, Σ).

      C     I MPLEMENTATION DETAILS
844
845   C.1    H ARDWARE
846
847   We base our experiments on a NVIDIA RTX A6000 GPU with AMD EPYC 7742 64-Core Processors.
848
849   C.2    T RAINING AND INFERENCE
850
851   We train our models for 200 epochs with the Adam optimizer (Kingma and Ba, 2017) and a
852
      learning_rate = 0.001, and test with the model corresponding to the lowest training loss.
      The size of our D = {20, 100} model is 4.87M and 4.89M parameters, respectively. We show the
853
      training process of PFNs and our model in Algorithm 1.
854
855
      Dealing with varying dimensions and dataset size For an input with d features, we follow Müller
856   et al. (2022) and deal with d < D by rescaling the input with D
                                                                    d and padding the features to size D
857   with 0, and randomly sample D features out of d if d > D. In addition, FoMo-0D uses context size of
858   5K at inference, where we randomly sample (5K−1) points as Dtrain from datasets with n > 5K for
859   each test sample x ∈ Dtest .
860
861   Model architecture We use a 4-layer Transformer with hidden dimension h_dim = 256, a linear
862   layer (RD → Rh_dim ) as the embedding layer and a 2-layer MLP (Rh_dim → R2 ) as the classification
863   layer for inlier vs. outlier. For each Transformer layer, we use num_head = 4 for each attention
      module and R = 500 for the router-based attention (Figure 2).


                                                            16
         Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


864
865
         Algorithm 1: Prior-fitting of a PFN (Müller et al., 2022) and ours
866      Input      :A prior distribution over datasets p(D), from which samples can be drawn and the number of
                     datasets Q to draw for one epoch, the number of training epochs E, the periodicity P , the number
867
                     of unique datasets q, linear transformation T .
868      Output :A model qθ that will approximate the PPD
869    1 Initialize the neural network qθ ;
870    2 Initialize the epoch-level collection CE = [ ];
871    3 for i ← 1 to E do
       4      if i ≤ P then
872
       5           Initialize an empty buffer Bi = [ ];
873    6           Initialize the dataset-level collection Cq = [ ];
874    7           for j ← 1 to Q do
875    8                if j ≤ q then
                                                                        |D    |
876    9                     Step 1: sample Dj := Dtrain ∪ {(xk , yk )}i=ktest ∼ p(D);
877   10                     Cq ← Cq + [Dj ]
878   11                end
      12                else
879
      13                     j ← j mod q
880   14                     Dj ← T (Cq [j])
881   15                end
                                                                             P|Dtest |
882   16                Step 2: compute stochastic loss approximation ℓ̄θ = k=1        (− log qθ (yk |xk , Dtrain ));
883   17                Step 3: update parameters θ with stochastic gradient descent on ∇θ ℓ̄θ ;
884   18                Bi ← Bi + [Dj ]
885   19           end
886   20           CE ← CE + [Bi ]

      22      else

920   “P=1”) across all 200 epochs, which is reasonable since the transformed datasets have non-diagonal
921   covariances that make the learning task harder and thus result in a higher training loss. The training
922   losses of FoMo-0D with D = 100 are also higher than with D = 20 since the subspace OD tasks are
923   harder in higher dimensions.

942
      D     D ETAILED E XPERIMENT S ETUP
943
944
      D.1     P RE - TRAINING DATASET S YNTHESIS
945
946   During pretraining, we generate unique GMM datasets by first drawing a configuration, including
947   dimensionality d ∈ [D], number of components m ∈ [M ], centers {µj }m                             d
                                                                                j=1 (each µj ∈ [−5, 5] )
948                          m                       d
      and covariances {Σj }j=1 (diag(Σj ) ∈ [−5, 5] ). We set M = 5 and vary D ∈ {20, 100} to study
949   pretraining with relatively small and high dimensional datasets, respectively. We synthesize inliers
950   and outliers as described in Section 3.1.
951
952
      We then sample S = 5, 000 points that are within the 90th percentile of the GMM. To synthesize
      outliers, we “inflate” a subset of dimensions by randomly choosing |K| ∈ [D] dimensions and
953
      multiplying the corresponding variances by ×5 (following (Han et al., 2022)), i.e. 5 × Σj,kk ’s for
954
      k ∈ K, and then draw S = 5, 000 samples from the inflated GMM that are outside the 90th percentile
955   of the original GMM.
956
957   To speed up data synthesis via linear transformations, we first draw 500 unique datasets using m ∈ [5]
958
      and d ∈ {1, 2, . . . , 100} (i.e. 5×100) and transform each one 15× using varying parameters (W, b)
      as described in Section 3.3.6 This yields 8K unique datasets (500 original and 7,500 transformed) to
959
      use at one training epoch (over 1,000 steps with batch size B = 8). We repeat this process at each
960
      epoch, drawing 500 new datasets and transforming them to reach 8K datasets per epoch.
961
962
963
      D.2     R EAL - WORLD B ENCHMARK DATASETS
964
      While pretraining is purely on synthetic datasets, we evaluate FoMo-0D on 57 real-world datasets
965
      from the ADBench benchmark (Han et al., 2022) (see Table 15). They consist of 47 popular tabular
966   outlier detection datasets, as well as 10 newly-constructed tabular datasets created from images and
967   natural language tasks by using pretrained models to extract embeddings. We defer to the original
968   paper for the details on these benchmark datasets.
969
          6
970         It is important to ensure that the eigenvalues of W (i.e. variances) are not too small such that the dataset does
971   not flatten in any direction. To this end, we draw a random orthonormal basis U ∈ [−1, 1]d×d and a diagonal Λ
      with eigenvalues λkk ∈ ([−1, −0.1] ∪ [0.1, 1])d , and obtain W = UΛUT . We also use b ∈ [−1, 1]d .


                                                               18
       Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


972
       We compare to DTE (Livernoche et al., 2024) and baselines therein as described next, thus, following
973    their semi-supervised OD setup we split each dataset five times into train/test using five different
974    seeds and report the mean performance and its standard deviation. In particular, each random split
975    designates 50% of the inliers as Dtrain , while Dtest contains the rest of the inliers and all the outlier
976    samples. Note that while the baseline methods require model re-training and inference for each
977    Dtrain /Dtest split, FoMo-0D uses the splits only for inference as Dtrain is merely passed as context.
978
       Before passing the datasets as input to FoMo-0D, we perform a quantile transform such that the
979    features follow a Normal distribution, to better align with the pretraining data from GMMs.
980
981
       D.3    BASELINES
982
983    We compare FoMo-0D against 26 baselines, from classical/shallow methods to modern/deep models.
984    Our baselines include all the baselines imported from one of the latest papers that proposed the SOTA
985    diffusion-based model DTE (Livernoche et al., 2024), and its three variants; DTE-C, DTE-IG, and
986    DTE-NP. Their baselines comprise all those in ADBench (Han et al., 2022); both classical ones
987    (kNN (Ramaswamy et al., 2000), LOF (Breunig et al., 2000), iForest (Liu et al., 2008), HBOS
988    (Goldstein and Dengel, 2012), etc.) and deep models (DeepSVDD (Ruff et al., 2018), DAGMM
989
       (Zong et al., 2018), DROCC (Goyal et al., 2020), etc.). They also include more recent approaches
       based on self-supervised learning (GOAD (Bergman and Hoshen, 2020), ICL (Shenkar and Wolf,
990
       2022), SLAD (Xu et al., 2023), etc.), besides the four additional generative baselines: normalizing
991
       planar flows (Rezende and Mohamed, 2015), DDPM (Ho et al., 2020), VAE (Kingma, 2013) and
992    GANomaly (Akcay et al., 2019). We defer to the original paper for additional details. Overall,
993    our 26 baselines consist of the most recent, SOTA approaches for OD that span a diverse family
994    (nonparametric, self-supervised, generative, etc.).
995

999    (from top to bottom) top-4 baselines.

1042
       E     A BLATION A NALYSES
1043
1044   In this section, we perform various ablations to study the effect of different design choices in FoMo-
1045   0D; namely, E.1 maximum pretraining data dimensionality D, the number of routers R on E.2 cost
1046
       and E.3 performance, E.4 context size (both for training and inference), E.5 number of unique
       datasets used for pretraining (i.e., reuse periodicity P ), data transformation T during synthesis on E.6
1047
       performance and E.7 speed up, E.8 data diversity and prolonged training, and finally, E.9 quantile
1048
       transforming the benchmark datasets preceding inference.
1049
1050   Unless stated otherwise, most ablation results are performed using FoMo-0D with D = 20, as it is
1051   faster to pretrain under these many varying settings.
1052
1053   E.1    E FFECT OF PRETRAINING DIMENSIONALITY D
1054
1055   How does FoMo-0D’s generalization performance change by increasing dimensionality of the
1056   pretraining data?
1057   We start by comparing FoMo-0D pretrained on datasets with up to D = 20 versus D = 100
1058   dimensions. Note that learning on higher dimensional datasets is harder, as evident from the relatively
1059   larger pretraining loss as shown in Appendix Figure 5. While the statement is accurate in general, it
1060   is also partly because subspace outliers “hide” better in higher dimensions.
1061
       Comparing Table 1 (D = 100) with Table 2 (D = 20) w.r.t. p-values over All datasets, we find that
1062
       FoMo-0D at larger scale does better, where all p-values are larger for D = 100 than D = 20. We
1063   find that FoMo-0D with D = 20 performs well on datasets with d ≤ 20 (i.e., “on its own game”),
1064   however beyond its pretraining setting, e.g. on datasets with d ≤ 50, D = 100 is superior to D = 20
1065   as shown in Appendix Table 8.
1066
1067
       E.2    E FFECT OF ROUTERS ON COST
1068
1069
       What is the running time and memory cost of FoMo-0D with & w/out router-based attention?
1070
1071   Figure 8(left) shows the average inference time per test sample, comparing FoMo-0D using a router-
1072   based attention mechanism with R = 500 routers (in green) versus FoMo-0D using typical attention
1073
       without any routers (in blue). As inference context size increases, running time for traditional
       attention grows quadratically while router mechanism scales linearly.7
1074
1075   Similarly, memory cost with routers is considerably lower when using routers, especially for larger
1076   context sizes, as shown in Figure 8(middle).
1077
1078       7
             Note that the inference time is reported on CPUs to show scalability. On GPUs, w/ 5K context size, see
1079   Appendix Figure 6, where typical attention takes advantage of parallelism (6.5ms), while router-based attention
       is slightly slower (7.7 ms w/ 500 routers) due to its two sequential self-attentions; see Eq.s (4) and (5).

       Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025

       E.3   E FFECT OF ROUTERS ON PERFORMANCE
1095
1096   What is the impact of the number R of routers (or representatives) on performance?
1097
       Router-based mechanism allows to trade-off running time with expressiveness of the attention and
1098
       hence performance.                                 (b)
                          (a) Figure 8(right) shows the p-values                         (c) test as the
                                                                 of the Wilcoxon signed rank
1099
       number of routers R is increased from 100 to 200 and 500, comparing FoMo-0D to each of the top-6
1100   baselines. We notice that FoMo-0D performance tends to increase monotonically with more routers.
1101
1102   E.4   E FFECT OF CONTEXT SIZE
1103
1104   What is the impact of context size, both during model pretraining as well as during inference?
1105
       To study how performance changes by context size, we train FoMo-0D with varying context size
1106
       in {1K,2K,5K} and employ each pretrained model for inference with varying context size in
1107   {1K,2K,5K,10K}. Table 5 shows the results, where performance is depicted by the average rank of
1108   FoMo-0D (the lower, the better).
1109
1110   Table 5: Average rank (based on comparison to 30 baselines w.r.t. AUROC) of FoMo-0D across
1111   datasets under different context sizes for training and inference. Smaller ranks imply better perfor-
1112   mance. (setting: D = 20, R = 500, P = 1)
1113                                      Infer:1K   Infer:2K   Infer:5K   Infer:10K
1114
                               Train:1K    13.816     14.623     15.193     15.439
1115                           Train:2K    13.079     13.219     13.439     13.561
1116                           Train:5K    13.088     13.211     13.307     13.430
1117
1118   We find that training with a larger context improves performance at any inference context size. On
1119   the other hand, perhaps counter-intuitively, FoMo-0D with smaller inference context size does better.
1120   We conjecture that is because the #routers-to-context size ratio increases with a larger context size
1121   at inference, limiting the expressive power of the “bottleneck” attention mechanism. The pairwise
1122   statistical tests among the 3 × 4 = 12 models support these observations, as shown in Figure 9.
1123   Interestingly, when training context size is large enough at 5K, inference with 10K samples generalizes
1124   beyond training with no significant difference (at 0.05) from other inference context sizes.
1125   E.5   E FFECT OF NUMBER OF UNIQUE DATASETS
1126
1127   How do FoMo-0D performances compare when pretrained on unique vs. reused datasets, via
1128   varying periodicity P ?
1129   Next we study the effect of dataset reuse at epoch level (w/out transformation) on performance as
1130   presented in Section 3.3. We vary reuse periodicity P in {1, 50, 100}, and accordingly, increase the
1131   number of unique datasets used for pretraining across epochs. As shown in Table 6, FoMo-0D (w/
1132   D = 20) performs similarly with varying dataset reuse. In fact, it is competitive even with P = 1,
1133   remaining no different from the 3rd best baseline (ICL) across All (57) datasets, while significantly
       outperforming the top 5th (LOF) across (24) datasets with d ≤ 20 as well as (38) with d ≤ 50.

       How do FoMo-0D performances compare when pretrained on datasets with vs. w/out linear

1173   where we compare reuse of the same 8K unique datasets across epochs (w/out T ), versus transforming

1175   stable; no different from the top 3rd model on All datasets, while significantly outperforming the top
1176
       5th across those with d ≤ 20 and d ≤ 50. This suggests that T can be employed without sacrificing

       E.9   E FFECT OF APPLYING QUANTILE TRANSFORM ON BENCHMARK DATASETS
1232
1233
       What is the impact of quantile data transform preceding inference on performance?
1234
1235   We pretrain FoMo-0D on synthetic datasets from a simple data prior based on GMMs. The real-
1236   world benchmark datasets, on the other hand, may exhibit features with distributions different from
1237   Gaussians. To close the gap, we apply a quantile transform (denoted QT) on the benchmark datasets
1238
       prior to feeding them to FoMo-0D for inference, which transforms the features to exhibit a more
       Gaussian-like probability distribution.
1239
1240   Figure 13 compares the performance of three FoMo-0D w/ D = 100 variants with and w/out QT
1241   against the top-5 baselines w.r.t. the p-values of the paired Wilcoxon signed rank test. FoMo-0D tends
       to perform better as suggested by larger p-values when QT is applied.

1321
1322   Tables 9.1& 9.2, 10.1 & 10.2, and 11.1 & 11.2 respectively show the AUROC, AUPR and F1 scores
1323   of the top-4 baselines, DTE-NP, kNN, ICL, and DTE-C as well as their corresponding avg model
1324   with the average performance across HPs, as listed in Table 4.
1325   Tables 12.1&12.2, 13.1&13.2, and 14.1&14.2 respectively show the AUROC, AUPR and F1 scores of
1326   all methods across all benchmark datasets. In all these tables, the last four rows show the avg_rank
